<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="7c843e1ac0cb433d819885b012774778"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7c843e1ac0cb433d819885b012774778">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ce56efd8-d321-4ac3-b137-6714e5ce4c11'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d140064771" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>An innovative crystal modulation strategy was proposed and implemented on a Mn-based cathode Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with highly exposed {010} active facets and reinforced Mn−O bond, facilitating enduring anionic redox activity with fast kinetics. The robust structural stability and zero-strain behavior with ultra-low volume variation (0.1 C: 1.75 %, 1 C: 0.31 %, 5 C: 0.04 %) are directly revealed via advanced nondestructive time-resolved operando 2D-XRD.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e9e30404-81f6-41f7-9180-f597b0d39714/anie202415450-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e9e30404-81f6-41f7-9180-f597b0d39714/anie202415450-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b45a6323-e4d1-41dd-879e-c4bfa8a62c3f/anie202415450-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e9e30404-81f6-41f7-9180-f597b0d39714/anie202415450-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d140065009" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Mn-based layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries with anionic redox reactions hold great potential for energy storage applications due to their ultra-high capacity and cost effectiveness. However, achieving high capacity requires overcoming challenges such as oxygen-redox failure, sluggish kinetics, and structural degradation. Herein, we employ an innovative crystal modulation strategy, using Mn-based Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a representative cathode material, which shows that the highly exposed {010} active facets enable an enhanced rate capability (119.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C) with fast kinetics. Meanwhile, the reinforced Mn−O bond inhibits excessive oxidation of lattice oxygen and O−O cohesion loss, stabilizing and maintaining a long-enduring reversible oxygen-redox activity (100 % high capacity retention after 100 cycles at 0.5 C and 84.28 % retention after 300 cycles at 5 C). Time-resolved operando two-dimensional X-ray diffraction reveals the robust structural stability, zero-strain behavior, and suppressed phase transition with ultra-low volume variation during cycling at different rates (0.1 C: 1.75 %, 1 C: 0.31 %, 5 C: 0.04 %). Additionally, the full cell coupled with hard carbon achieves a high energy density of approximately 211 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> with superior performance. This work highlights the significance of crystal modulation and presents a universal approach in developing Mn-based oxide cathodes with stable anionic redox for high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415450-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415450-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The integration of clean and low-carbon energy sources, including wind and solar power, into green transportation and renewable grids is rapidly expanding.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0001_R_d140064762e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as sustainable alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale energy storage applications, addressing resource scarcity concerns associated with high cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0004_R_d140064762e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Among the various cathode materials, layered transition metal (<i>TM</i>) oxides stand out for their high theoretical specific capacity and straightforward synthesis process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0007_R_d140064762e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Manganese (Mn)-based or Mn-rich oxides are particularly attractive due to their cost effectiveness and environmental friendliness, as well as their potential to reduce the reliance on nickel (Ni) and cobalt (Co) in cathodes, thereby enhancing market competitiveness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0011_R_d140064762e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> By activating the oxygen anionic redox reaction (OAR) at high operating voltages, Mn-based oxide cathodes can achieve multi-electron transfer, leading to additional specific capacities exceeding 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and ultra-high energy densities of over 900 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> level.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0015_R_d140064762e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> However, the achievement of high capacity severely suffers from the poor OAR kinetics of Mn-based oxides, especially during cycling at high rates, which is essential for regulating Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics to enhance OAR activity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0019_R_d140064762e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span> Additionally, this OAR activation often leads to irreversible lattice oxygen loss and O<sub>2</sub> release, causing structural degradation and decreased cycle life.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0020_R_d140064762e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Therefore, ensuring structural stability is also of great importance.</p>
         
         <p>As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0001">1a</a>, multi-dimensional perspectives have identified several obstacles for the final commercialization of Mn-based layered oxides with high anionic redox.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0024_R_d140064762e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Extensive modification efforts, including atomic-level doping, structural design, interface engineering via micro- and nano-coatings, and electrolyte modulation, have been undertaken to address critical challenges such as oxygen-redox failure, capacity degradation, and poor kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0027_R_d140064762e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Despite certain breakthroughs and advances, previous studies have not yet identified a comprehensive method to significantly improve rate performance, maintain long-term OAR, and ensure capacity retention.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0032_R_d140064762e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Most research only focuses on one specific aspect, such as optimizing Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics at the expense of overall performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0036_R_d140064762e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Within the framework of reliable material design,<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0005_R_d140064762e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2a - Science">2a</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0016_R_d140064762e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Joule">5a</a></sup></span> reasonable crystal modulation and morphology design are favorable to improve reaction kinetics, ensuring the preservation of theoretical capacity while enhancing performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0040_R_d140064762e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> Moreover, the crystal modulation offers much more design flexibility, allowing for targeted customization to meet specific performance demands, such as exposing crystal {010} active facets, creating special microsphere structure, synthesizing micro-sized single-crystal particles and designing other one-/three-dimensional (1D/3D) morphologic structures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0030_R_d140064762e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9c - Chem. Eng. J.">9c</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0038_R_d140064762e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11b</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0043_R_d140064762e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></sup></span> Hence, the needs-based structural design for crystal customization affords a new outlook for resolving the obstacles and optimizing performances of Mn-based oxide cathodes with OAR activity.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2215bf5-15c7-4916-957d-975cc7ddee3e/anie202415450-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2215bf5-15c7-4916-957d-975cc7ddee3e/anie202415450-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c237ff78-9882-45dd-ac58-5e8e2b827773/anie202415450-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2215bf5-15c7-4916-957d-975cc7ddee3e/anie202415450-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Multi-dimensional analysis towards advantages and challenges in Mn-based oxide cathodes with high anionic redox. (b) Crystal structure of NLM with inset arrows showing various migration pathways of Na<sup>+</sup>. (c) Theoretical surface energy of the {010} and {001} facets. Comparison of Na atom migration barrier in different orientations: (d) Path A and Path B, (e) Path C and Path D.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Herein, inspired by the aforementioned discussions and theoretical calculations, we propose and implement an innovative crystal modulation strategy for Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLM), a representative state-of-the-art Mn-based cathode with ultra-high specific capacity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0016_R_d140064762e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Joule">5a</a></sup></span> Benefiting from the modulation characteristic of primary particle self-templated growth, the as-modulated Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (HNLM), featuring highly exposed {010} active facets and reinforced Mn−O bond stability, was successfully prepared by a facile surfactant-assisted hydrothermal method. Systematic analysis and multi-dimensional comparison reveal an intrinsic correlation among crystal modulation and structural stability, OAR reversibility, and reaction kinetics. The optimized HNLM cathode displays a high reversible discharge specific capacity performance (242.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, 1 C=200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), exhibiting long-lasting reversible OAR activity (100 % capacity retention after 100 cycles of 0.5 C, 2.0–4.5 V) and faster kinetics along with enhanced rate performance (119.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C high rate), which extends its cycle life (84.28 % capacity retention after 300 cycles of 5 C, 1.5–4.5 V). The full cell using the HNLM cathode also achieves a superior energy density of approximately 211 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, along with improved cycling and rate performance. The stabilization of the oxygen-redox reaction is achieved by inhibiting excessive oxidation of lattice oxygen and preventing O−O cohesion loss through the reinforced Mn−O bond. Time-resolved operando two-dimensional X-ray diffraction (2D-XRD) demonstrates that the HNLM exhibits zero-strain characteristic and suppressed phase transition, with ultra-low volume variation during cycling at various rates (0.1 C: 1.75 %, 1 C: 0.31 %, 5 C: 0.04 %). This work presents a simple and general pathway with broad applicability for designing the ecofriendly Mn-based oxide cathodes with reversible OAR and superior rate capabilities.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415450-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415450-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To investigate the internal structure-performance relationship of layered NLM oxide, ab initio first-principal density functional theory (DFT) was firstly employed to analyze the effects of oriented growth and diffusion kinetics from surface and bulk (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0001">1b</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0044_R_d140064762e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Phys. Rev. B">14</a></sup></span> This analysis demonstrates that the surface energy of different crystal facets and corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> migration energies along various crystal orientations are crucial factors.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0045_R_d140064762e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The calculated surface energy (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0001">1c</a> and Table S1) for NLM reveals that it is 3.61 J m<sup>−2</sup> for the {010} facets, approximately twice that of the {001} facets, which is 1.82 J m<sup>−2</sup>. Surface energy can greatly influence the single-crystal nucleation and growth during sintering process, thereby affecting the distribution of particles’ facets.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0048_R_d140064762e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span> The {001} facets with low surface energy dominate continuous growth during sintering, naturally leading to a higher proportion compared to {010} facets. This plays a pivotal role on the reaction kinetics and OAR reversibility of the material during de-/sodiation process, particularly regarding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in different directions within the bulk and near-surface regions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0049_R_d140064762e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>A surface model exposed with {010} facets was constructed to study the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy in two directions (Figure S1), which is particularly effective for understanding surface behavior in processes such as catalysis, adsorption, and ion migration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0050_R_d140064762e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0001">1d</a>, the migration energy for Path A (9.262 eV) along the [001]-direction is significantly higher than that for Path B (4.251 eV) at a similar distance, demonstrating that migration of Na<sup>+</sup> through dense <i>TM</i> layer is practically unattainable. The 1D Path B (Na<sup>+</sup> migration from internal sites within the bulk to sites near exposed {010} facets surface) along [010] direction serves as an active pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, thus establishing the {010} facets as the active facets. Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0001">1e</a>, the migration energy near the surface (0.184 eV, Path D) is much lower than that for the internal migration (0.326 eV, Path C), manifesting that Na<sup>+</sup> near the surface is more readily for de-/sodiation. This observation highlights the significance of the migration path distance, emphasizing the importance of controlling the exposure of active facets (corresponding to the thickness H of lateral plane) and the length of migration paths (representing the dimension L of top plane) to enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of cathode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0038_R_d140064762e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11b</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Guided by theoretical calculations, single-crystal HNLM with highly exposed {010} facets and reduced {001} facets length was synthesized under the help of cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) surfactant and self-template growth to enable faster and more efficient Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2a</a>. In this method, CTA<sup>+</sup> cationic groups preferentially adsorb on the lateral surfaces of hexagonal plates which are negatively charged, suppressing the spread growth of the low-energy {001} plane effectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0047_R_d140064762e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15b - Ceram. Int.">15b</a></sup></span> Subsequently, the hydroxide precursors grow along <i>c</i>-axis accompanied by the <i>Ostwald ripening</i> process,<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0053_R_d140064762e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> forming thinner nanosheets (Figure S2a). These thinner nanosheets undergo pre-oxidation calcination. During this crucial process, the interlayer hydrogen bonds between hydroxyl groups break, leading to dehydration and an increase in O−O interlayer repulsion. Meanwhile, the oxidation of Mn further increases its valence, enhancing the Mn−O bond strength and shortening the MnO<sub>6</sub> intralayer spacing, thus stabilizing the oxide lattice structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0056_R_d140064762e852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> Concurrently, the isotropic growth of the nanosheets promotes along the <i>c</i>-axis for stacking into oxide self-template structure with a higher proportion of {010} facets (Figure S2b). The increased O−O repulsion also contributes to a larger interlayer spacing, facilitating subsequent lithiation/sodiation during high-temperature calcination.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0059_R_d140064762e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mol. Model.">21</a></sup></span> Then multiple self-templates assemble into thicker cylinder-shaped HNLM with highly exposed {010} facets and efficient Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/74dde86f-c0ef-4cdb-9b53-a0a565381deb/anie202415450-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/74dde86f-c0ef-4cdb-9b53-a0a565381deb/anie202415450-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05c896c8-2503-4097-890c-323668ad72b9/anie202415450-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/74dde86f-c0ef-4cdb-9b53-a0a565381deb/anie202415450-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Graphical synthesis routes of HNLM. XRD patterns with Rietveld refinement results of (b) HNLM and (c) SNLM. (d) Schematic crystal structures and lattice parameter changes of as-prepared two samples. SEM images of (e, f) HNLM and (g, h) SNLM at different magnifications. (i) Schematic illustration of characteristic dimensions: {010} facets thickness (<i>H</i>) and the {001} facets dimension (<i>L</i>). (j) TEM images of HNLM. HRTEM images of HNLM along (k) [010] axis and (m) [001] axis, and the inset at the top-left corner: FFT corresponding to lattice fringes. SAED pattern of HNLM in different axes: (l) [010] axis and (n) [001] axis.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The fundamental differences between the HNLM and the sample synthesized via solid-state method (SNLM) are that regulated oxide self-template has enabled precise modulation on crystal facets and interlayer (<i>d</i><sub>(O−Na−O)</sub>)/intralayer (<i>d</i><sub>(O−<i>TM</i>−O)</sub>) spacings,<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0060_R_d140064762e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> while the pre-oxidation evolution process has modulated the Mn−O bond lengths through different oxide precursors. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2b</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2c</a> and Tables S2, S3, all characteristic peaks of XRD for the as-prepared two samples are sharp and well defined, fitting to the standard <i>P</i>63/<i>mmc</i> space group of P2 phase without additional peaks, which confirms well-crystallized and pure phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0016_R_d140064762e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Joule">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0019_R_d140064762e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span> The <i>Rietveld</i> refined lattice parameters shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2d</a> suggest that the HNLM has a higher interlayer spacing (<i>d</i><sub>(O−Na−O)</sub>=3.393 Å) than that of the SNLM (3.003 Å), which would facilitate the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>. Additionally, the <i>d</i><sub>(O−<i>TM</i>−O)</sub> for the HNLM and SNLM show values of 2.130 Å and 2.513 Å, and the average Mn−O bond lengths in <i>TM</i> layer are refined to be 1.966 Å and 2.078 Å, respectively. This can strengthen the bonding between Mn and O atoms, providing better stabilization for lattice oxygen and Na−O−Li configuration.</p>
         
         <p>The morphological and structural analyses of the HNLM and SNLM reveal significant differences in their particle shapes and dimensions. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figures <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2e</a>, 2 f, and S3a) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2j</a>) show that the HNLM consists of uniform single-crystal particles with a cylindrical shape and a smooth surface. In contrast, the SNLM primarily consists of hexagonal thin plates that tend to agglomerate, as shown in Figures <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2g</a>, 2 h, and S3b. The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings (Figures S4 and S5) and inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometer (ICP-OES) results (Table S4) confirm that both the HNLM and SNLM have homogeneous element distributions. The particle dimension distribution analysis (Figure S6) indicates that the HNLM particles have an average thickness of approximately 1.5 μm, while the SNLM particles are thinner, averaging around 0.5 μm. This results in an <i>H</i>/<i>L</i> ratio of 1 : 1.07 for the HNLM, reflecting a higher proportion of {010} active facets (65.1 %) compared to that of the SNLM, which has an <i>H</i>/<i>L</i> ratio of 1 : 5.6 and {010} active facets proportion of only 26.3 %. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images, combined with fast Fourier transform (FFT) refinement (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2k</a> and 2 m) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2l</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0002">2n</a>), further corroborate these observations. The HRTEM images reveal well-defined parallel and hexagonal bright fringes corresponding to lattice spacings of 5.60 Å and 2.59 Å, which are attributed to the (002) and (100) planes, respectively. These findings confirm the hexagonal crystal structure of the HNLM and highlight its distinct structural features compared to that of the SNLM.</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the impact of crystal modulation on electrochemical performance, cycling and rate charge/discharge measurements were performed on the prepared HNLM and SNLM cathodes in half cells, with an initial activation at 0.1 C for the first three cycles. The voltage curves for the HNLM and SNLM across certain cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3b</a>) depict initial reversible discharge capacities of 242.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 251.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, within 1.5–4.5 V cut-off voltage, which indicates the inheritance of high capacity from the high OAR of NLM material (272 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 C but showing noticeable decline).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0016_R_d140064762e985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Joule">5a</a></sup></span> Notably, the HNLM and SNLM exhibit the capacity retentions of 82.0 % and 70.8 % after 100 cycles at 0.5 C, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3b</a>). By reducing the voltage range to 2.0–4.5 V, the HNLM exhibits exceptional capacity retention of 100 %, with a reversible discharge capacity of 141.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3c</a>). It is evident in associated <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> curves that the HNLM maintains relatively high OAR reversibility and overlapped redox peaks during cycling (Figure S7a). In comparation, the SNLM cathode deteriorates from 128.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> to 75.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> together with continuously diminished redox peaks in Figure S7b. Particularly, the oxygen redox peak is no longer prominent accompanied by severe voltage fade, which is the main drawback for Mn-based layered cathodes involving oxygen redox at high voltage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0033_R_d140064762e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10a</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0034_R_d140064762e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a></sup></span> This amelioration observed in the HNLM can be attributed to the reinforced Mn−O bond inherited from oxide precursor for a more efficient mixing of Mn center with oxygen orbitals, which inhibits the excessive oxidation and O−O cohesion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0067_R_d140064762e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> Thus, the crystal modulation can significantly stabilize lattice oxygen and inhibit oxygen loss at high operating voltage, as well as suppress the disproportionation reaction of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>, as demonstrated in previous studies.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0018_R_d140064762e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Nat. Commun.">5c</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0028_R_d140064762e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9a - Adv. Mater.">9a</a></sup></span> Regarding the SNLM, the Mn−O bond is stretched during deep desodiation at high voltage, which facilitates the release of lattice oxygen, leading to unavoidable structure distortion. Moreover, as can be seen in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure S8), two pairs of distinct peaks (approximately 2.5 V and 4.2/4.0 V) are observed due to the active Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and 2O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sub>2</sub><sup>n−</sup> (1≤n≤3) redox couples, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0034_R_d140064762e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0069_R_d140064762e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24b</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/88179877-2de1-429c-a381-27ca6518f993/anie202415450-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/88179877-2de1-429c-a381-27ca6518f993/anie202415450-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/80bb6823-50f1-4424-bd0b-a1c216d7ce08/anie202415450-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/88179877-2de1-429c-a381-27ca6518f993/anie202415450-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of (a) HNLM and (b) SNLM cathodes at 0.5 C in the cut-off voltage of 1.5–4.5 V. (c) Cycling performance of both cathodes at 0.5 C over 100 cycles within 2.0–4.5 V. (d) Rate capabilities of HNLM and SNLM at 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C within 1.5–4.5 V. (e) Long-term cycling performance of both cathodes at 5 C over 300 cycles during 1.5–4.5 V.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The rate capability of the HNLM was further assessed from 0.2 C to 10 C within 1.5–4.5 V (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3d</a>). Remarkably, it demonstrates superior output that delivers specific capacities of 207.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 185.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 169.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 145.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and 119.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (from 0.2 C to 0.5 C, 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C), respectively. A capacity of 198.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be swiftly regained upon reverting to 0.2 C, indicating the robustness of the structure in facilitating rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport. In contrast, the SNLM exhibits a sharp decline with significant fluctuations as C rate progressively increases. The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) plot of the HNLM by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) demonstrates a smooth and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability during charging/discharging (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>=8.93×10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, over three times than that of the SNLM, Figure S9). Specifically, the HNLM has an extended 2O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sub>2</sub><sup>n−</sup> redox plateau at high voltage than the SNLM during the 2<sup>nd</sup> charging process. Meanwhile, there is a common inflection point of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at approximately 3.95 V for both the two cathodes, since the anionic redox kinetic is slower compared to the cationic one during charge.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0071_R_d140064762e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span> Thus, it is crucial to enhance the OAR reaction kinetics at this rate-determining step (3.95–4.5 V). The average <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the HNLM is approximately 10 times higher than that of the SNLM in high voltage region (Figure S10), indicating a significantly decreased energy barrier for OAR reaction. Afterwards, the <i>Nyquist</i> plots measured by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements elucidate that the refined values of <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for the HNLM are significantly smaller than those of SNLM (Figure S11a and Table S5). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient refined by <i>Warburg</i> impedance <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> shows a consistent trend with the GITT results, further confirming the superior Na<sup>+</sup> transport of the HNLM (Figure S11b). This achievement can be largely attributed to the optimization of crystal structure and the notable difference in active facets proportion (e.g., 38.8 % between the HNLM and SNLM), as well as widened interlayer spacing.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0062_R_d140064762e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22b - ACS Energy Lett.">22b</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the HNLM cathode maintains a brilliant long lifespan under a fast-cycling rate of 5 C in 1.5–4.5 V, with a reversible capacity of 95.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a reasonable capacity retention of 84.28 % after 300 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0003">3e</a>). There is a comprehensive capability comparison between the crystal-modulated HNLM and other reported anionic redox induced layered cathodes evaluated under various conditions (Table S6 for high rates and Table S7 for the same cut-off voltage). This analysis reveals a significant improvement of the HNLM in cycling capacity and retention, as well as high-rate capability vs. counterparts.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0069_R_d140064762e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24b - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24b</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0071_R_d140064762e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span> To further understand the internal reason, EIS experiments were immediately conducted on the cells after cycling for 300 cycles. The cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) resistance <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for the SNLM listed in Figure S12 and Table S8 are two times higher than those of the HNLM, which remain nearly unaffected even after long-term cycling. This suggests that the SNLM undergoes severe structure degradation and accumulation of interface reaction by-products, which will be further explored in the subsequent verifications of CEI film and surface structure evolution.</p>
         
         <p>As revealed by the time-resolved operando 2D-XRD patterns of the HNLM and SNLM cathodes in Figures <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0004">4</a> and S13–S17, both the two cathodes maintain the P2 phase structure throughout charge and discharge under various conditions (0.1 C activation, 1 C for 3 cycles, and 5 C for long cycles within 1.5–4.5 V cut-off voltage). During the initial activation at 0.1 C (Figures <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0004">4a</a> and S13), neither the two cathodes show disappearance nor splitting of characteristic diffraction peaks as the voltage reaches 4.5 V. For the HNLM, which benefits from crystal modulation, the diffraction peaks corresponding to (002), (100), (102), (103), and (104) remain unchanged upon charging to 4.5 V. Slight shifts in the (100), (102), and (103) peaks are noted only upon discharging to 1.5 V. Conversely, the SNLM shows notable changes: expansion along the <i>c</i> axis and contraction in the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> axes, evidenced by the shifts of (100) and (102) peaks toward higher 2<i>θ</i> angles (Figure S18a and S18b). This structural stability of the HNLM is reflected in its volume variation (▵<i>V</i>) after the first cycle at 0.1 C, which remains relatively low at 1.75 %, compared to 3.29 % for that of the SNLM and most other reported layered cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0071_R_d140064762e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span> The significant volume change in the SNLM leads to decreased intensity and broader half-peak widths of the (103) and (104) peaks during subsequent high-rate cycles. When the rate is increased to 1 C (as shown in Figures S15, S16, and S19), the SNLM demonstrates significant volume deformation (▵<i>V</i>=2.61 %), which is directly linked to its poorer rate performance and irreversible capacity loss during prolonged cycling at high rates. In contrast, the HNLM exhibits a much smaller volume deformation (▵<i>V</i>=0.31 % at 1 C), indicating that it maintains a nearly zero-strain structural evolution throughout the cycling process. Furthermore, the operando XRD patterns for the 1<sup>st</sup> and 5<sup>th</sup> cycles at 5 C are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0004">4c</a> to investigate the structural stability of the HNLM under fast charge/discharge conditions, and the variations in lattice parameters over the cycles at 5 C for both the HNLM and SNLM are displayed in Figure S20. Remarkably, the HNLM shows an exceptionally low volume deformation (▵<i>V</i>=0.04 % after the first 5 C cycle, compared to 0.60 % for the first cycle and 1.82 % for SNLM), underscoring its remarkable zero-strain behavior (compared with other reported works shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0004">4d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0064_R_d140064762e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23a - Nat. Commun.">23a</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0065_R_d140064762e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23b - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23b</a></sup></span> This minimal volume change accounts for the HNLM′s superior rate capability and extended cycle life, highlighting its effectiveness in high-rate applications.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3be232e1-9b78-4ded-9f65-896e7a7dc584/anie202415450-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3be232e1-9b78-4ded-9f65-896e7a7dc584/anie202415450-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/67c9ebe5-452e-4a1f-b764-a64256890027/anie202415450-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3be232e1-9b78-4ded-9f65-896e7a7dc584/anie202415450-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Time-resolved operando 2D-XRD characterization of HNLM during the first charge/discharge process at 0.1 C. (b) Variation of cell volume corresponding to the 0.1 C process. (c) Structural evolution and cell volume variation for HNLM at 5 C. (d) Comparison of capacity and volume variation with other reported layered cathodes.<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0063" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0063_R_d140064762e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span></p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To confirm the structural stability of the HNLM and its resistance to irreversible phase transitions at high voltage, an atomic-scale analysis was performed on the samples charged initially to 4.5 V, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5b</a>. The high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF, highlighting <i>TM</i> with bright dots) and annular bright-field (ABF, displaying Na and O between the <i>TM</i> layers) spherical aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy (Cs-STEM) images (with interpretative marked diagram in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5b</a>) clearly reveal a well-defined atomic arrangement with P2 structure. Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5c</a>, further analysis using geometric phase analysis (GPA) of the HAADF-STEM image was employed to map the strain distribution in different directions, including <i>ϵ<sub>xx</sub></i>, <i>ϵ<sub>yy</sub></i>, <i>ϵ<sub>xy</sub></i>, and <i>R<sub>xy</sub></i>. The strain distribution appears relatively uniform with values near zero, especially for <i>ϵ</i><sub>xy</sub> and <i>R<sub>xy</sub></i>, indicating the isotropic behavior of the HNLM even after deep Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. This observation is supported by electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) images (Figure S21), which reveal no signs of lattice defects, expansion, or strain distortion. The results suggest that the superior structural stability of the crystal-modulated HNLM material can be attributed to its ability to maintain the P2 phase structure and exhibit zero-strain characteristic during cycling.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/81bdbc38-e9db-4388-a119-a3eff6f16a2d/anie202415450-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/81bdbc38-e9db-4388-a119-a3eff6f16a2d/anie202415450-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca4ec461-efdb-445e-b746-9c453c687196/anie202415450-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/81bdbc38-e9db-4388-a119-a3eff6f16a2d/anie202415450-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) HAADF image and (b) ABF image of HNLM at the charged to 4.5 V state obtained by the Cs-STEM. (c) GPA results of HAADF-STEM image in different orientations. The SEM images of (d, e) HNLM and (f, g) SNLM cathodes at different magnifications. The TEM and HRTEM images of (h, i) HNLM and (j, k) SNLM cathodes after 100 cycles at the rate of 0.5 C, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further understand the microscopic structural evolution of cycled electrodes, SEM and HRTEM analyses were performed on the samples that underwent 100 cycles at 0.5 C, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5d</a>–<a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">k</a>. For the cycled HNLM cathode, the SEM images reveal that the integrity of the single-crystal particles is well preserved (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5d</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5e</a>). There is no noticeable volume contraction or expansion, and the particle surfaces are smooth, with no observable cracks on the {010} facets. In contrast, the SNLM cathode shows significant surface degradation. The SEM images indicate the presence of side-reaction products and numerous cracks on the {010} facets, leading to considerable volume expansion (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5f</a> and 5 g). It is evident in the ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results that the SNLM requires more Mn<sup>4+</sup> reduction for charge compensation (Mn<sup>4+</sup> : Mn<sup>3+</sup>=32 : 68 lower than 48 : 52 for the HNLM) due to the loss of oxygen activity, resulting in the lattice distortion attributed to the <i>Jahn–Teller</i> effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions (Figures S22 and S23).</p>
         
         <p>The HRTEM images provide further insights into the structural integrity of the materials. Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5j</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5k</a> show that the SNLM particles are encased in a much thicker CEI film compared to the HNLM in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5h</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5i</a>. This thicker CEI film significantly impedes Na<sup>+</sup> transport and reaction kinetics, contributing to reduced performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0073_R_d140064762e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small Methods">27</a></sup></span> Attributed to the reinforced Mn−O bond and zero-strain characteristic, the lattice fringe on the [010] plane of the HNLM maintains a well-distributed pattern (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5i</a>), which enables a long-lasting reversible OAR activity. The ex situ X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra of Mn <i>K</i>-edge for the HNLM indicate the well-maintained coordination and crystal symmetry of the HNLM and the effective suppression of lattice distortion linked to <i>Jahn–Teller</i> effect or migration of Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions (Figure S24).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0068_R_d140064762e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24a - Nat. Commun.">24a</a></sup></span> Regarding the SNLM, it is much more complex. The formation of disordered phases and surface defects due to the severe interface side reactions during long cycling deteriorates the particle integrity and OAR reactivity causing severe capacity decay and huge voltage hysteresis (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0005">5k</a>). As illustrated in the ex situ XPS results for both the two cathodes charged to 4.5 V after different cycles, the OAR reversibility of the HNLM is largely maintained over long-term cycles compared to that of the SNLM which declines continuously (Figure S25). This is consistent with the aforementioned <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> analysis, where the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> dominates the charge compensation of the SNLM during long cycling. Then the accumulation of excessive Mn<sup>3+</sup> leads to unfavorable <i>Jahn–Teller</i> distortion, disproportionation reaction and structural degradation in the SNLM.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the practical application potential of the HNLM, comprehensive full cell investigations were conducted on the rational coupling of the HNLM cathode with commercial hard carbon (HC) anode (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6a</a>) by adjusting the capacity ratio (N/P ratio) to 1.25 and setting the cut-off voltage to 1.6–4.3 V (determined by CV results) (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6b</a> and <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6c</a>). Initially, the HC anode was examined in the voltage range of 0.01–2.0 V and displayed a stable reversible capacity of 331.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 70 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6b</a>). The HNLM//HC full cell exhibits an initial discharge capacity of 233.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the cathode) with an energy density of approximately 211 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the active mass of both cathode and anode) at 0.1 C. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6d</a>, the HNLM//HC full cell demonstrates superior rate performance compared to the SNLM//HC full cell, with capacities of 106.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 75.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C and 10 C, respectively. Moreover, the HNLM//HC full cell showcases outstanding cycling stability (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6e</a>), retaining 81.79 % of its capacity at 0.5 C after 50 cycles. Figure S26 illustrates its charge/discharge curves during long-term cycling, showing no significant voltage decay. The <i>Ragone</i> plot, derived from the rate performance data and based on the combined mass of electrodes, depicts the relationship between energy density and power density in Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6f</a> (energy densities of 198.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, 171.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, 139.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, 121.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, and 92.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at power densities of 71.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, 178.6 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, 349.4 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, 678.6 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, and 1553.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). These results highlight the competitive performance of the HNLM//HC full cell device in comparison to recently reported works (Figure <a href="#anie202415450-fig-0006">6f</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0066_R_d140064762e1435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23c - Energy Storage Mater.">23c</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0072_R_d140064762e1435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202415450-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/22ac0270-8766-49f1-90b5-7860f675128b/anie202415450-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/22ac0270-8766-49f1-90b5-7860f675128b/anie202415450-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3edb53b5-d82a-40c6-9da1-34a990137e64/anie202415450-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/22ac0270-8766-49f1-90b5-7860f675128b/anie202415450-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415450-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415450" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic diagram of the full cell employing NLM cathode and HC anode. (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves and (c) CV curves for HNLM cathode (red), HC anode (blue), and HNLM//HC full cell (yellow). (d) Rate performance and (e) Cycling performance at 0.5 C with an inset of the lighting sign-“NCEPU” for the full cells. (f) <i>Ragone</i> plot of HNLM//HC full cell and comparison with recently reported works.<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#anie202415450-bib-0066" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0066_R_d140064762e1462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23c - Energy Storage Mater.">23c</a>, <a href="#anie202415450-bib-0072" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415450-bib-0072_R_d140064762e1462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span></p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415450-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415450-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we successfully designed and prepared a Mn-based cathode Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with highly exposed {010} active facets by a facile surfactant-assisted hydrothermal method. The resulting single-crystal HNLM displayed a large interlayer spacing, reinforced Mn−O bonds, and a shortened Na<sup>+</sup> migration path, facilitating enduring anionic redox activity with fast kinetics. Consequently, this material achieved a high reversible capacity (242.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C), and enhanced rate capability (119.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C high rate). Time-resolved operando 2D-XRD demonstrated the robust structural stability of the material, with zero-strain characteristic and ultra-low volume variation at different rates (0.1 C: 1.75 %, 1 C: 0.31 %, 5 C: 0.04 %), indicating long-lasting reversible oxygen-redox activity. Investigations into the redox evolution during long-term cycling revealed that charge compensation in the SNLM primarily shifted to Mn<sup>4+/3+</sup> couples due to the loss of oxygen-redox activity. This shift led to an accumulation of excessive Mn<sup>3+</sup>, resulting in unfavorable <i>Jahn–Teller</i> distortion and interface side reactions. This study elucidates the intrinsic relationship of crystal modulation, structural stability, OAR reversibility, and reaction kinetics, providing a novel pathway for designing Mn-based oxide cathodes with reversible anionic redox and superior rate performance, which holds broad applicability for future cathode material development.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
